Be SPECIFIC — include the patient's diagnosis, cancer type, and the purpose of this visit.

diagnosed metastatic ER+ breast cancer" or "cycle 3 day 1 of irinotecan for metastatic TNBC").

If something is redacted (*****), write "[REDACTED]" — do NOT guess or fill in.
If biopsy has not been done, do NOT call a mass "cancer" — call it a "mass" or "lesion".
If a specific test name is not mentioned, do NOT assume which test (e.g., do not assume "Oncotype Dx").

For Patient type — determining "New patient" vs "Follow up":
- "New patient" if ANY of these apply:
  * Note says "New Patient Evaluation", "Consult Note", "consultation", "establish care", "referred by", or "second opinion"
  * This is the patient's FIRST visit to this oncologist/clinic (even if they had surgery or treatment elsewhere)
  * Post-surgery first visit to medical oncology to discuss adjuvant/systemic therapy
- "Follow up" ONLY if the patient has been seen by THIS provider/clinic before for ongoing management
- When in doubt, "New patient" is safer than "Follow up"

For second opinion — write "yes" ONLY if the note explicitly mentions "second opinion", "another opinion", or the patient was clearly referred for an independent review of their case. Do NOT guess.

Do NOT write just "breast cancer" or "MBC" — always include the subtype, receptor status, grade, and special features when available in the note.
IMPORTANT: Search the ENTIRE note for receptor status — it may appear in PMH, Problem List, Pathology, HPI, Assessment/Plan, or Diagnosis section, not just the Assessment. If ER/PR/HER2 status is stated ANYWHERE, include it. Do NOT omit HER2 status just because it appears only in the Past Medical History.
IMPORTANT: If the Assessment/Plan says "HR+" or "hormone receptor positive", this means ER+ (and usually PR+). If it says "her 2 negative" or "HER2-" or "*****-", this means HER2 NEGATIVE. Do NOT write "not specified" when the A/P explicitly states receptor status.

HER2 status rules — follow strictly:
- If the note explicitly states HER2 result (positive, negative, equivocal, IHC score, FISH result), write it.
- If HER2 was NOT tested or NOT mentioned in the note, write "HER2: not tested" — do NOT guess or assume HER2- or HER2+.
- DCIS (ductal carcinoma in situ) often does NOT have HER2 testing. Do NOT assume HER2- for DCIS unless explicitly stated.
- HER2 IHC scoring — use this table to determine HER2 status:
  IHC 0     = HER2 NEGATIVE
  IHC 1+    = HER2 NEGATIVE
  IHC 2+    = EQUIVOCAL — must check FISH/ISH result:
    IHC 2+ with FISH ratio ≥ 2.0 (or gene copies ≥ 6) = HER2 POSITIVE
    IHC 2+ with FISH ratio < 2.0 (or "FISH negative")  = HER2 NEGATIVE
  IHC 3+    = HER2 POSITIVE
- FISH interpretation (when reported without IHC):
  FISH ratio ≥ 2.0 = amplified = HER2 POSITIVE
  FISH ratio < 2.0 = not amplified = HER2 NEGATIVE
- IMPORTANT: If treatment includes Herceptin/trastuzumab/pertuzumab/T-DM1/T-DXd/Tykerb/lapatinib/TCHP/THP/ado-trastuzumab, the cancer is HER2+. Do NOT write HER2-.
- IMPORTANT: If the note says "triple negative" ANYWHERE (HPI, A/P, diagnosis), that means ER-/PR-/HER2-. Write HER2 as NEGATIVE even if the HER2 IHC/FISH data is [REDACTED] or masked. "Triple negative" is a definitive classification.
- IMPORTANT: If HER2 IHC/FISH data is [REDACTED] but the Assessment/Plan writes "[REDACTED] negative" in the receptor status line, infer HER2 = negative.
- IMPORTANT: goserelin (Zoladex) / leuprolide (Lupron) used DURING CHEMOTHERAPY for fertility preservation does NOT indicate ER+. These drugs suppress ovaries to protect eggs during chemo. Only infer ER+ from goserelin/Lupron when it is used as ENDOCRINE THERAPY (adjuvant or palliative, continuous long-term use for hormone suppression). If the note says "goserelin for fertility preservation" or "goserelin during chemotherapy", do NOT add "ER+ (inferred from goserelin)".
- IMPORTANT: If the patient has MULTIPLE primary cancers (e.g., gastric cancer AND breast cancer), report receptor status ONLY for the BREAST cancer. Do NOT mix receptor status from other cancers. For example, if gastric cancer is HER2+ but breast cancer is HER2-, write HER2- for the breast cancer.
- TNBC CONSISTENCY CHECK: If you classify the cancer as "TNBC" or "triple negative", the receptor status MUST be ER-/PR-/HER2-. Do NOT write "TNBC" together with "ER+" or "PR+" — that is a contradiction. If the cancer was reclassified from HER2+ to TNBC after surgery, the FINAL classification (TNBC) takes priority. Write: "ER-/PR-/HER2- (TNBC; initially classified as HER2+ but reclassified after surgical pathology)"
- RECEPTOR STATUS PRIORITY — when multiple biopsies exist:
  If the patient has had MULTIPLE pathology results (core biopsy, surgical specimen, metastatic biopsy), the MOST RECENT pathology takes priority for current receptor status.
  Always report the CURRENT receptor status first, then note the original if different.
  Example: If core biopsy was ER+/PR+/HER2- but surgical specimen after neoadjuvant shows ER+/PR-/HER2-, write: "ER+/PR-/HER2- (post-neoadjuvant; originally PR+)"
  If metastatic biopsy shows different receptors from the primary, the metastatic biopsy is the CURRENT status.
- HISTOLOGIC SUBTYPE — do NOT confuse imaging with pathology:
  "lobular mass" or "lobulated mass" on imaging describes the SHAPE of the mass, NOT the histologic subtype.
  Only write "invasive lobular carcinoma" (ILC) if pathology explicitly says "lobular carcinoma" or "ILC".
  If pathology says "adenocarcinoma" or "adenoCA" without specifying ductal or lobular, write the exact pathology term (e.g., "Grade III adenocarcinoma"), NOT "invasive lobular carcinoma".
- REDACTED HER2 — inference rules:

  If the Problem List says "ER+PR+ *****-", the "*****" is HER2 and the "-" means NEGATIVE.
  Only write "HER2: not tested" when there is genuinely NO mention of HER2 testing or results anywhere in the note AND no treatment context to infer from.

For Stage_of_Cancer:
- Write the AJCC/TNM stage if stated (e.g., "Stage IIA", "pT2N0M0", "Stage IV")
- If the patient was originally diagnosed at one stage but now has metastatic recurrence, write BOTH:

  IMPORTANT: Only write "Originally Stage [X]" if the note EXPLICITLY states the original stage number (e.g., "Stage IIA at diagnosis"). If the note only says "metastatic recurrence" without specifying the original stage, write just "Metastatic (Stage IV)" — do NOT guess or invent the original stage.
- If distant metastases are documented, the current stage is Stage IV even if not explicitly stated.
- If the Assessment/Plan states a stage (e.g., "Stage I", "early stage"), extract it — do NOT write "Not mentioned in note".
- If tumor size and node status are available, ALWAYS infer the approximate stage — do NOT write "Not mentioned" when you have these data:
  - ≤2cm (T1) + N0 = Stage I (or Stage IA)
  - ≤2cm + N1mi (micromet only) = Stage IB or Stage IIA
  - >2cm ≤5cm (T2) + N0 = Stage IIA
  - >2cm ≤5cm + N1 (1-3 LN+) = Stage IIB
  - >5cm (T3) + any N = at least Stage IIIA
  - 4+ LN+ (N2) = at least Stage IIIA
  - Distant metastasis (M1) = Stage IV
  Example: "0.8 cm IDC, 0/5 LN" → write "Stage IA (inferred from pT1b N0)"
  Example: "2.2 cm, 4/19 LN+" → write "Stage IIIA (inferred from pT2 N2a)"
- CRITICAL — N classification is based on the NUMBER OF POSITIVE nodes, NOT total nodes examined:
  N1 = 1-3 positive nodes (N1a if macrometastases, N1mi if only micrometastases)
  N2 = 4-9 positive nodes
  N3 = 10+ positive nodes
  Example: "3/28 LN positive" = 3 positive out of 28 examined = N1a (NOT N2)
  Example: "5/12 LN positive" = 5 positive = N2a
  Do NOT confuse total nodes examined with number positive.
- IMPORTANT: Node-negative ("node neg", "0/N LN+") with small tumor (≤2cm) = Stage I, NOT Stage II.
  Stage II requires either positive lymph nodes OR tumor >2cm. "Early stage node negative" = Stage I.
- Only write "Not mentioned in note" if there is truly NO staging, tumor size, or node information anywhere in the note.

', 'Response_Assessment': 'TASK: Extract how the cancer is CURRENTLY responding to treatment.

Think step by step before answering:
1. Has this patient started any cancer treatment yet? (surgery alone does not count as "treatment" for response assessment purposes)
   CRITICAL EXCEPTION — POST-NEOADJUVANT SURGERY: If the patient received neoadjuvant therapy (chemo, targeted therapy, etc.) BEFORE surgery, the surgical pathology IS the response assessment. Describe the pathologic response:
   - Residual tumor size and cellularity (e.g., "3.84 cm residual tumor, 5% cellularity")
   - Lymph node status post-treatment (e.g., "3/28 LN positive with treatment effect")
   - Whether pathologic complete response (pCR) was achieved or not

   IMPORTANT: Completed radiation therapy COUNTS as treatment. If the patient recently finished radiation, they HAVE received treatment — do NOT write "Not yet on treatment".    IMPORTANT: A patient who was on PREVIOUS treatment and now shows disease PROGRESSION or RECURRENCE — that IS a response assessment (the response is "progression"). Even if they are switching to a new regimen or between treatments, document the current disease status from imaging/labs.

Do NOT write "cancer is not responding to treatment" — the patient may have discontinued treatment or completed it years ago. Focus on WHAT happened (recurrence), not WHY.
   IMPORTANT: For patients on adjuvant/maintenance therapy, "no evidence of recurrence" or "no evidence of disease" on exam or imaging IS a valid response assessment — it means the treatment is working.
   IMPORTANT — the patient IS on treatment if ANY of these clues appear:
   - "currently on [drug]", "taking [drug]", "continue [drug]", "on [drug] since [date]"
   - Mentions of chemotherapy cycles, off weeks, dose adjustments (e.g., "off week", "cycle 3", "due to start next cycle")
   - "labs okay to proceed with chemotherapy" or similar pre-cycle clearance
   - Current medications include any oncologic drug (chemo, hormonal, targeted, immunotherapy)
   - Active medication list contains chemotherapy drugs (Xeloda, ixabepilone, paclitaxel, etc.)
   Do NOT be confused by "off week" — a patient on an off week between chemo cycles IS on treatment.
2. If yes, what treatment are they on? (chemo, endocrine therapy, targeted therapy, immunotherapy)
3. Is there any evidence of how the cancer is responding? Search the ENTIRE note for ANY of these:
   - Imaging: CT stable, PET response, scan results
   - Tumor markers: CA 15-3, CEA rising or falling
   - Physical exam: mass shrinking, "breaking up", palpable changes, skin changes improving
   - Clinical status: "labs okay to proceed" (implies stable enough to continue)
   - Symptom changes: "able to do more", "pain improving", "up and about"
   Even brief exam comments like "area appears to be breaking up" count as response evidence.
4. If no treatment started yet, write "Not yet on treatment — no response to assess."
5. If on treatment but no response data in the note, write "Not mentioned in note."
   BEFORE writing "Not mentioned", verify there is truly NO evidence anywhere in the note. These ALL count as response evidence:
   - "stopped [drug] for progression" or "disease progressed on [drug]" → response is PROGRESSION
   - New metastatic sites (brain, lung, liver, bone mets) → response is PROGRESSION
   - "exam stable" or "no new findings" → response is STABLE DISEASE
   - Tumor marker trends (rising or falling)
   - Post-surgical pathology after neoadjuvant therapy
   If ANY of the above is present, do NOT write "Not mentioned" — describe the evidence.
6. TEMPORAL ATTRIBUTION — critical rule:
   When citing imaging (PET, CT, MRI, bone scan), verify the scan was done AFTER the current treatment regimen started.
   If the patient switched treatments (e.g., from letrozole to Faslodex), a scan done BEFORE the switch reflects the PRIOR treatment's response, not the current one.
   Example: PET on 10/10 showed progression → patient switched to Faslodex on 10/16 → current visit exam says "stable" → the response on CURRENT treatment is "exam stable", NOT "PET showed progression on Faslodex."
7. "JUST PRESCRIBED" ≠ "ON TREATMENT":
   If a medication was PRESCRIBED or ORDERED at THIS visit (e.g., "Rx for letrozole given", "prescription ordered", "will start", "instructed to begin"), the patient has NOT yet started treatment. Write "Not yet on treatment — no response to assess."
   "On treatment" requires the patient to have ACTUALLY BEEN TAKING the medication for some time.
   Clues that treatment has NOT started: "will begin", "Rx given today", "instructed to start", "prescription ordered", "ok to start", "she can start immediately"
8. A/P ASSESSMENT TAKES PRIORITY:
   If the A/P section contains an explicit current assessment like "Exam stable", "doing well", "NED", "no evidence of recurrence", or "no evidence of disease", prioritize THIS over historical imaging data from earlier in the note.
   The A/P represents the physician's CURRENT assessment at this visit. Historical imaging in the HPI/Problem List may be from BEFORE the current treatment regimen.

STRICT RULES — do NOT violate:
- Do NOT include future plans ("will start", "plan to", "she will have radiation").
- Do NOT include treatment discussions or recommendations.
- Do NOT use generic boilerplate phrases. Every claim must be traceable to a specific sentence in the note.
- Do NOT write "tolerating therapy well" or "symptoms improved" unless the note EXPLICITLY uses those exact words.
- Do NOT use the word "responding" or "response" unless you can cite SPECIFIC imaging or tumor marker evidence. "Labs okay" or "stable on exam" alone does NOT mean the cancer is "responding" — it means the cancer status is unknown or stable.
- IMPORTANT: "responding" implies MEASURABLE IMPROVEMENT. Use "stable disease" for unchanged findings, "no evidence of disease" for no measurable disease, and "responding" ONLY when there is documented tumor shrinkage or marker decrease.
- Do NOT describe pathology results (tumor size, grade, margins) as a "response" — pathology is a diagnosis, not a treatment response.
- Do NOT describe surgical recovery ("healing well", "recovering from surgery") as a response assessment.
- Do NOT describe risk estimates or Oncotype scores as a response — those are prognostic tools, not treatment responses.
- Do NOT write "Not applicable" or "Not specified".
Examples:
- GOOD: "CT stable, CA 15-3 decreased from 28 to 14"
- GOOD: "no evidence of disease recurrence on imaging and exam"
- GOOD: "PET-CT shows stable to slightly decreased metabolic activity of osseous metastases"
- GOOD: "Axillary LN decreased from 8cm to 2-3cm after 2 cycles AC"
- GOOD: "Disease progressed on neoadjuvant AC/T, proceeded to bilateral mastectomies. Surgical pathology showed residual 2.5cm tumor with 5/12 positive lymph nodes."
- GOOD: "Stopped neoadjuvant therapy due to progression. S/p mastectomy with residual disease."
- BAD: "tolerating therapy well, symptoms improved" (generic boilerplate)
- BAD: "She will continue on letrozole and have radiation" (this is a plan, not a response)
- BAD: "0.9 cm grade 1 IDC, margins negative" (this is pathology, not treatment response)
- BAD: "Healing well from surgery" (surgical recovery, not cancer treatment response)
- BAD: "Low Risk Oncotype score" (prognostic tool, not a response to treatment)
- BAD: "Not mentioned in note" when the note says "stopped chemo for progression" or describes new brain/lung/bone metastases — these ARE response evidence (progression)
- BAD: "Not yet on treatment" for a patient who completed neoadjuvant chemo + surgery — the surgical pathology IS the response
- BAD: "PET showed progression on Faslodex" when the PET was done BEFORE Faslodex was started — wrong temporal attribution

Write your reasoning first, then output the JSON on a new line starting with {
IMPORTANT: Your JSON must contain ONLY the key "response_assessment". Do NOT add any other keys.

Respond with a JSON object using this exact schema (NO other keys):
{
"response_assessment": "How is the cancer CURRENTLY responding? Be DETAILED — cite specific evidence including: organ names, measurement changes (e.g., '1.8cm→1.2cm'), SUV values, specific imaging modality and date. Do NOT give vague summaries. Do NOT include future plans, surgical recovery, or prognostic scores."
}

"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a follow-up visit regarding your breast cancer treatment.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYour chest wall is more tender, red, and swollen, which might be due to an infection. Your back pain has gotten worse, possibly because the cancer has progressed. An MRI of your lower spine shows that the cancer has spread to your bones, especially around your S1 vertebra. You also have low levels of sodium and potassium in your blood. Your anemia, caused by the cancer and chemotherapy, is getting worse. You have a history of exposure to hepatitis B, but your current tests show no active infection.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nDue to side effects, your irinotecan treatment schedule has been changed to every other week, and the dose has been increased. You will continue to take medications for nausea, pain, and restless leg syndrome. Your Effexor-XR dosage has been increased . You have also been prescribed doxycycline for an infection.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou need to see a radiation oncologist for a consultation. You will have scans again in 3 months, due in September 2019. You will also have an MRI of your brain if your headaches get worse. Your blood tests for liver function and hepatitis B will be checked every 4 months. You are referred to social work for additional support. Your next clinic visit is in 2 weeks.\nWe understand that this is a challenging time and that managing these health changes can be stressful. We want to reinforce that you are not alone in this process.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent video visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a follow-up visit regarding your breast cancer treatment.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have undergone bilateral mastectomies with involvement in the right breast. The pathology results show a 3.84 cm invasive ductal carcinoma with negative margins. One lymph node showed macrometastases (0.21 cm) with extranodal extension, one lymph node showed micrometastases (0.025 cm), and one lymph node showed isolated tumor cells. The estrogen receptor is positive in 85% of tumor cells, while the progesterone receptor is negative. HER2 is negative by FISH. No in situ or invasive carcinoma was noted in the left breast. You currently have drains still in place and expanders.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou will continue to take supportive medications such as ondansetron (Zofran) and prochlorperazine (Compazine) for nausea.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou are referred to radiation therapy. Letrozole will be started after radiation therapy. Fosamax will be used for bone protection. Your drains are to be removed on Thursday. You are considered a full code.\nWe understand that this is a challenging time and that managing these health changes can be stressful. We want to reinforce that you are not alone in this process.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a new patient evaluation and second opinion regarding your breast cancer treatment.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have a history of left DCIS in 1994 and right stage II IDC in 2000. In May 2020, you had a metastatic relapse with a right chest wall recurrence, bone metastases, and contralateral infraclavicular and IM nodes on the right. The cancer is HR+ and HER2 negative. Recent PET scans showed a good response, and your physical exam revealed no palpable masses, skin changes, or lymphadenopathy. You developed pneumonitis due to abemaciclib in July 2021, leading to its discontinuation and the start of steroids.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nAbemaciclib was held since August 14, 2021, due to pneumonitis. Letrozole was switched to anastrozole in July 2021 due to a skin rash. A medication dose was reduced, and you have been on since October 2020. You are currently on anastrozole and Xgeva. Supportive medications include prednisone and Lomotil.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will have a PET CT now. If the PET CT shows stable disease, you will continue on anastrozole alone. If the PET CT shows progression, you could use faslodex with a medication if you have a specific mutation. Future options include hormone blockade with Afinitor or Xeloda or a clinical trial. Your next clinic visit will be after the PET CT results to determine the next steps. You are on full code status.\nWe understand that this is a challenging time and that managing these health changes can be stressful. We want to reinforce that you are not alone in this process.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have"

We discussed her original DCIS diagnosis from 2007 as well as a second biopsy in 2013 that was concerning for microinvasive disease; she did not undergo conventional therapy at the time of her original diagnoses.  I \n explained to her that she now had clear evidence of invasive breast cancer, but noted that her PET/CT demonstrated no evidence of metastases and therefore she can now be treated with curative intent.  I discussed with ***** and her husband in great detail the natural history and treatment of early stage *****-/*****+ IDC.  I explained that were she to forego the recommended therapy, I am very concerned that she would develop metastatic disease in the short term, even in the context of her 10 year history of DCIS.    We discussed the multimodality approach to breast cancer treatment. The patient understands that her treatment recommendations will include chemotherapy, biological therapies (trastuzumab and pertuzumab), surgery, and radiation. We discussed the advantages of offering chemotherapy in the neoadjuvant setting in order to increase the chances of breast conserving surgery, while concurrently allowing us to assess the response to treatment.  I explained that in the context of her high-risk node-negative, ***** positive disease we would typically recommend that she receive either 12 cycles of weekly paclitaxel plus trastuzumab/pertuzumab (THP) followed by 4 cycles of doxorubicin plus cyclophosphamide (AC) every 2 weeks or 6 cycles of every 3 week docetaxel, carboplatin, trastuzumab, and pertuzumab (TCHP).  I also explained that either regimen would be followed by every 3 week trastuzumab for a total of one year of biological therapy.     We reviewed the potential side effects of both regimens.  Given her extensive disease even in the absence of nodal involvement, I recommend that she proceed with an anthracycline-containing regimen, unless she is found to have substantial cardiac dysfunction on TTE.  I specifically discussed with ***** the typical and less common side-effects associated with THP/AC, such as: fatigue, nausea, myelosuppression, nail changes, alopecia, diarrhea, and constipation. I stated that \n with regards to trastuzumab, she is at risk of a reduced left ventricular ejection fraction, which can result in asymptomatic or symptomatic heart failure with dyspnea and chest pain.  I stated that pertuzumab is associated with diarrhea and rash.  We additionally discussed the rare adverse events associated with THP/AC, both secondary leukemia and cardiomyopathy.      After an extensive discussion, the patient completely understands the rationale for neoadjuvant treatment and will spend the weekend considering where she wants to proceed with her therapy.  I explained to her that I would recommend she start her treatment within 2 to 3 weeks.  She would need a TTE, Mediport placement, and if treated here at *****, a chemotherapy teaching session.    The patient will contact me after the weekend and let me know whether she plans to proceed with treatment here at ***** or elsewhere, closer to home.    I spent a total of 120 minutes face-to-face with the patient and 115 minutes of that time was spent counseling regarding the diagnosis, the treatment plan, the prognosis, medication risks, lifestyle modification, the risks and benefits of surgery, symptoms and therapeutic options.    \n"

--- Column: letter ---
"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a consultation regarding your newly diagnosed breast cancer to discuss treatment options and establish care.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have been diagnosed with a type of breast cancer called invasive ductal carcinoma, which started in the milk ducts. The cancer is also associated with another type called ductal carcinoma in situ, which is a non-invasive form of the disease. The cancer does not have the proteins called estrogen or progesterone receptors but does have a protein called HER2. The cancer is in your right breast and has not spread to other parts of your body.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou will start neoadjuvant chemotherapy, which is treatment given before surgery to shrink the cancer. The chemotherapy will be either 12 cycles of weekly paclitaxel plus trastuzumab/pertuzumab (targeted therapy with chemotherapy) followed by 4 cycles of doxorubicin plus cyclophosphamide (AC) every 2 weeks or 6 cycles of every 3 week docetaxel, carboplatin, trastuzumab, and pertuzumab (a combination of chemotherapy and targeted therapy drugs). This will be followed by every 3 week trastuzumab for a total of one year of biological therapy.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will need a TTE (echocardiogram, a heart ultrasound) before starting treatment. You will also have a Mediport placed, which is a device used to deliver medications. You are referred to for surgery and radiation as part of your treatment plan. The patient will contact the care team after the weekend to decide whether she plans to proceed with treatment at a medication or elsewhere.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a second opinion regarding your locally advanced breast cancer treatment.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have a type of breast cancer called TNBC (which stands for triple-negative breast cancer). This means the cancer does not have certain proteins that some other types of breast cancer have. The cancer started in the gland cells and is classified as grade III, which means it grows quickly.\nThe cancer is locally advanced, meaning it has grown within the breast but has not spread to distant parts of your body.\nAfter treatment with chemotherapy and surgery, there is still some cancer remaining, but it has not spread to the lymph nodes.\nYour physical exam shows that the area where the surgery was done is healing well, though there is some swelling in your legs and some tingling due to the chemotherapy.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nThe dose of your chemotherapy was reduced by 25% after the first cycle due to side effects.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou are scheduled to receive radiation therapy.\nYou are recommended to have genetic counseling and testing.\nIf further testing confirms that you need it, you may resume trastuzumab and continue with pertuzumab.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

Recent imaging shows that the cancer has progressed in your brain and liver.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou have started taking a medication called capecitabine, which you take one week on and one week off. You were also taking letrozole and palbociclib, but these were stopped in early July because the cancer had progressed.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will continue taking capecitabine at . You may also start low-dose steroids to reduce swelling and improve your symptoms. You will have a repeat lumbar puncture to check for cancer cells in your spinal fluid. You will also have a repeat CT scan of your chest, abdomen, and pelvis, and an MRI of your spine to check for cancer spread. You are referred to radiation oncology to consider radiation therapy. If the cancer continues to progress, you may be given a medication called fulvestrant along with another medication based on your genetic testing results. Your recent blood tests show that your thyroid stimulating hormone is a bit high, which might mean you have hypothyroidism. You also have a slightly swollen area in your armpit, but there’s no redness or drainage. You’re experiencing palpitations, constipation, mild nausea, and headaches. Your white blood cell count is higher than normal, and you have a low red blood cell count, which could mean you have anemia.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYour treatment plan includes continuing with cycle 4 of AC chemotherapy in 1 week, with a delay of 1 week to help you recover from side effects. The dose of GCSF has been reduced to 50%. You will also start taking granisetron, PO dex, and olanzapine to help with nausea. You will continue using lidocaine patches and analgesics as needed. You will continue taking gabapentin.\n**What is the plan going forward?**"

The patient's breast cancer history began in early 2009 when she was diagnosed with a locally advanced left-sided breast cancer. According to the notes provided by the referring physician, the invasive cancer was estrogen receptor positive, progesterone receptor positive, and HER-2/neu negative.  The patient completed 4 cycles of Adriamycin and Cytoxan followed by 4 cycles of Taxol given in a dose dense fashion in December 2008. Based on the increasing chest pain being experienced by the patient, I am highly suspicious that the lung lesion is progressing further, and the subpleural thickening in the anterior left lower lobe likely represents progression of disease as well.  At this point, in order to make the most appropriate treatment recommendations, I fully agree with the ***** medical oncology group that the patient needs to have a biopsy of either the pulmonary nodule, mediastinal adenopathy, or the area of subpleural thickening that might represent another site of metastatic disease.  I explained to the patient that I do not know what is the ***** approach to biopsy one or more of these lesions, and that we will need a followup CT scan which has contrast and thin slices in order to better understand the extent of disease, since the CT scan from 08/18/2012 was noncontrast and used for anatomic localization only.  In the meantime, I have submitted a referral to both interventional radiology as well as thoracic surgery to determine what is the best route for biopsy of her disease.    The patient understands that I cannot make a comprehensive treatment recommendation until I know the biological characteristics of her metastatic disease.  If her cancer remains estrogen receptor positive, it would be reasonable to consider ongoing hormonal therapy, but most likely combined with a biological therapy such as the combination of Exemestane and Afinitor. We would also entertain the possibility of a chemotherapy such as Xeloda which could have reasonable efficacy as well as allow the patient to maintain a decent quality of life. Alternatively, if the metastatic disease is hormone receptor \n negative, we will need to switch to chemotherapy to control her disease.  We will also discuss a variety of clinical trials combining combinations of chemotherapy with biological therapy.    In terms of symptom management, recent episodes of chest pain have been extremely intense for the patient, and she certainly needs a comprehensive review of pain management strategies.  I have placed a referral to our symptom management service so that they can focused their attention entirely on symptom management while I sort out what is the best treatment for her metastatic cancer. He will return to clinic in approximately 2 weeks, and hopefully by that time she will have had the chest CT, seen the thoracic surgeon, and her need by interventional radiology.    Patient ready and able to be educated, verbalizes understanding of information/instructions given.  Treatment options, side effects, exercise, and nutrition reviewed in detail.  Total face to face time: 100 min  Total counseling time: 90 min          \n"

--- Column: assessment_and_plan ---
"Assessment and Plan:  ***** ***** is a 49 y.o. female with a stage III left-sided breast cancer diagnosed in 2009, and a stage I right-sided breast cancer incidentally diagnosed at the time of prophylactic mastectomy in 2011. The patient was then diagnosed with metastatic disease in June 2012 based on imaging findings including mediastinal adenopathy and a left lung mass.  The patient has not \n had a pathologic diagnosis of metastatic disease, but was instead started on treatment in the form of fulvestrant.  A followup scan in August 2012 showed increase in size of the lung lesion but modest decrease in the size of the mediastinal lymphadenopathy.  Based on the increasing chest pain being experienced by the patient, I am highly suspicious that the lung lesion is progressing further, and the subpleural thickening in the anterior left lower lobe likely represents progression of disease as well.  At this point, in order to make the most appropriate treatment recommendations, I fully agree with the ***** medical oncology group that the patient needs to have a biopsy of either the pulmonary nodule, mediastinal adenopathy, or the area of subpleural thickening that might represent another site of metastatic disease.  I explained to the patient that I do not know what is the ***** approach to biopsy one or more of these lesions, and that we will need a followup CT scan which has contrast and thin slices in order to better understand the extent of disease, since the CT scan from 08/18/2012 was noncontrast and used for anatomic localization only.  In the meantime, I have submitted a referral to both interventional radiology as well as thoracic surgery to determine what is the best route for biopsy of her disease.    The patient understands that I cannot make a comprehensive treatment recommendation until I know the biological characteristics of her metastatic disease.  If her cancer remains estrogen receptor positive, it would be reasonable to consider ongoing hormonal therapy, but most likely combined with a biological therapy such as the combination of Exemestane and Afinitor. We would also entertain the possibility of a chemotherapy such as Xeloda which could have reasonable efficacy as well as allow the patient to maintain a decent quality of life. Alternatively, if the metastatic disease is hormone receptor \n negative, we will need to switch to chemotherapy to control her disease.  We will also discuss a variety of clinical trials combining combinations of chemotherapy with biological therapy.    In terms of symptom management, recent episodes of chest pain have been extremely intense for the patient, and she certainly needs a comprehensive review of pain management strategies.  I have placed a referral to our symptom management service so that they can focused their attention entirely on symptom management while I sort out what is the best treatment for her metastatic cancer. He will return to clinic in approximately 2 weeks, and hopefully by that time she will have had the chest CT, seen the thoracic surgeon, and her need by interventional radiology. --- Column: letter ---
"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a new consultation regarding your breast cancer treatment.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou were diagnosed with a type of breast cancer that started in the milk ducts (grade 3 invasive ductal carcinoma) and has spread to other parts of your body, including your left lung and lymph nodes in the middle of your chest.\nA recent PET/CT scan showed that the cancer in your lymph nodes has slightly decreased in size, but the cancer in your lung has grown larger.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou recently switched from anastrozole to letrozole because of joint pains.\nYou are currently taking fulvestrant.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will need a biopsy of either the cancer in your lung or the lymph nodes in your chest to understand more about the cancer.\nYou will also have a followup CT scan with contrast and thin slices to get a better picture of how the cancer is growing.\nDepending on the results of the biopsy, you may continue with hormonal therapy or switch to chemotherapy. Clinical trials combining chemotherapy with biological therapy are also being considered.\nYou are referred to a symptom management service to help with pain and other symptoms.\nYou will return to clinic in approximately 2 weeks to review the chest CT, consult with a thoracic surgeon, and assess the need for interventional radiology.\nWe understand that this is a challenging time and that managing these health changes can be stressful. We want to reinforce that you are not alone in this process.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"
